Although Not Your Kind of People does not break much new ground sonically, it is classic Garbage, and it fills a gaping void in the current musical landscape.  Listeners will experience bouts of thrashing, crunching, guitar-driven euphoria (see: \\"Control,\\" \\"Battle in Me,\\" and \\"Man on a Wire\\").  Listeners will also become entranced by hypnotic beats (\\"Sugar\\") and atmospheric dreaminess (\\"Not Your Kind of People\\").<br /><br />Ultimately, what separates Not Your Kind of People from its predecessors is its thematic range.  One expects a heavy dose of rage and sullenness on a Garbage record, and it's there. Additionally, Shirley's interpretation of \\"This Little Light of Mine\\" on \\"Beloved Freak\\" achieves a similar vibe.<br /><br />Without a doubt, Not Your Kind of People revels in and rivals the glory of Garbage's earliest work, notably Version 2.0.  A long-time fan, I could not be more satisfied with Not Your Kind of People.  Five beaming stars out of five.

0440244412|5.0|Shadowfever review with major SPOILERS:::::!!!!!!<br /><br />I'm going to do my best to keep them to a minimum but there are a few things you should know going into this.<br /><br />1. Don't read Shadowfever if you haven't read the other four books IN ORDER. It's pointless and it will dilute if not ruin the journey for you. It is worth the wait. Go pick up a few paperbacks or order them online at amazon, zip through them then buy this book The pay off is worth it.<br /><br />2. You probably, shouldn't read this review if you haven't read the book yet. I'll do my best to be general but I don't want to ruin anything for you. If you haven't read the book, and you still read the review and something is spoiled for you...you were warned.<br /><br />--------------------------------<br /><br />I can't remember having anticipated a book this much. This was a remarkably satisfying journey, filled with great tension, action, and passion. ShadowFever has twists, and turns, and stunning twists again. In the past with other books before they came out, I have wanted to read spoilers.<br /><br />With Shadowfever, I didn't want to know a thing before I had the book in my hands and I'm glad I didn't because there were so many surprises, it would have (for me at least ) taken away from my joy had I known ahead of time. Just when you would think you have something figured out, another twist.<br /><br />Given the cliffhanger at the end of Dreamfever, the first 90 plus pages of this very large tome were difficult to read. I struggled mightily to get through them. I don't think it was necessary to drag out the suspense that long as to whether Barrons was dead or not. I don't think it was necessary to have Mac align herself with Darroc. It created such a miserable situation when Barren's did come back. Then the person we've thought of as the main villain, was disposed of in a shockingly quick, rushed and downright odd manner before the book was 1/4 of the way over. I did not see that coming.<br /><br />Moning however certainly took us on a journey. I don't think it is a huge spoiler to state upfront that yes....Mac and Barron's get their HEA. When Moning herself stated in an interview that no character that she has created has obsessed her as much as Barrons, I thought it unlikely that he would have not survived. His timing for his return however...sucked. I HATED that part. But it was good for the story I guess. This is what i mean, there were things that were so hard to read but you couldn't stop.<br /><br />Personally, in suspense, when lovers are (at least to me the reader/viewer)...unnecessarily kept apart for \\"the good of the story\\", when just some straight talk and honesty could cut the crap and they could work together to save the day, it is frustrating. At times I felt...jeez...we are back to where we were three books ago. The scenes with Mac and Barron's sizzle...but they are both lying to each other, or not divulging the truth, he's only showing he cares by displaying jealousy....and she's off at the Abbey, or off on a beach with V'lane etc instead of asking what to me would be some of the obvious questions that don't come up until the very end.<br /><br />But wow, when it's right. It is so right. The conversations between Barrons/Mac, both spoken and unspoken, are tense, hilarious, and sexy. And the scenes between them near the end are seriously worth five books of waiting. Never again though. I don't think I can handle it.<br /><br />There appears to be a division in the ranks of the fans as to whether the real Villain of the piece could be \\"redeemed\\". To me, it is a nonstarter, based on the Rape. End of story. If someone wants to discuss it with me in comments I'm willing to go there but I have no interest in reading in later books one where he is brought back and the love of a good woman saves him. I wish him dead. End of story.<br /><br />There also seem to be some of the fans of the Highlander series who keep talking about how disappointed they are that Barrons and Mac didn't \\"say the words of the Druidic commitment ceremony\\". I loved those books but they were ROMANCE Novels. The Fae books are closer to Horror then Romance and there would be no room for that in this series. They don't even need to tell eachother they love eachother so to have these two people suddenly start spouting loving words like that would have been insulting to the characters involved and the readers who have gone on this exciting yet exhausting 5 book journey.<br /><br />I feel for readers of romance who have followed an author and then can't seem to make the switch to Urban Fantasy, make that mental adjustment when their favorite authors do. I'm sure it is frustrating, if you expect one thing and suddenly are getting something else. You can always see them, pick them out as the dissatisfied fan in the Amazon book reviews and the forums who whine about how it isn't like the romance novels and basically I discount their opinion. That sounds harsh but there it is. Like or dislike a book based on the rules of the genre, not on your preconception from another series in another genre.<br /><br />I do however have some tiny bloody bones to pick. Moning's not telling the fans who or what Barrons is, the whole...\\"I can't tell you or I'd have to kill you thing\\" feels contrived. It is another unnecessary frustrating cliffhanger obviously intended to hook people into reading the next three books Moning has been signed to write in the series. They will focus on Christian, Ryodan, and Dani. Obviously she wants readers to buy the next books to find out the answers to those questions.<br /><br />I don't understand Mac's being so cold to Christian and leaving him in the silvers so long before getting help to get him out and who gets him out still makes no sense to me given further developments. There is also a scene that is not at end I felt would have been the resolution of the entire series that is inexplicably missing. Obviously it will be in the next series, but to me anyway...it is the wrong choice. Besides the whole Barrons/Mac/Vlane triangle, we've also been reading to find out killed Mac's sister Alina. We find out the who, but we are left hanging on how that knowledge will be handled. Mac tells us how she will handle it, and we aren't shown.<br /><br />On another note, alot of people seemed to find the character of Dani annoying, I thought she was great. I\\"m not sure why all the \\"Dani hate\\" in the Amazon Forums. I don't know that I'm ready to read a book featuring her until she grows older, but I loved her and Mac hanging out together.<br /><br />So many things were answered that I would have thought it impossible to wrap up all those threads in such a satisfying manner; who killed Alina and why, who the real villain of the piece was and why, who the Unseely King was and where he'd been hiding,(although the \\"why\\" of that wasn't ever really explained) how Mac knew the things she knew.<br /><br />Moning really did create a near masterpiece of Urban Fantasy and I will re-read it over and over. Much like Moning, I'm obsessed with Barrons and although at times I was frustrated with Mac, I loved her and think she did pretty damn well for a 24 year old who started out knowing only that her sister had been murdered and she wanted justice. It turned out in the end, justice for Alina's death would actually involve deep forgiveness, and Alina's death was only the beginning.<br /><br />Shadowfever, really is a book about shadow selfs. Facing what is deep inside and what is the truth of ourselves as well as others, and really that is as true in real life as it is in Fantasy, which is part of what makes something so fantastical, feel so real.I loved those books but they were ROMANCE Novels. B002VECM4A|5.0|Gabourey Sibide, as Precious, is every bit as good as I've heard. The script has some problems, but not with the part of Precious. Her character is inspirational without being unrealistic. We like Precious, we feel empathy for her character and her circumstances, but we're not manipulated into liking her.<br /><br />The only problem I have with this film - and it's not really the fault of the film - is that it seems to focus all of the \\"blame\\" on the mother and father. I think that this was a dramatic decision that was made, and it wasn't meant to ignore all the other factors which create the kind of environment that kids like Precious live in. When a movie depicts a rough life in the ghetto, that doesn't mean it has to get into all of the complicated stuff that creates ghettos in the first place. Nor does it have to get into what we, as a society, could do to help kids who are trapped in toxic environments. It's all too easy to hate and blame the parents, but for ignoring the rest of it I was going to give this movie three or four stars. After I thought about it, I saw this film as a powerful slice - a small slice - of a big problem that we have in America. I realized that the narrow perspective, the narrow focus, also distilled the power of this drama. This is a powerful drama, not a comprehensive documentary.<br /><br />Some critics see this film as depressing, stereotyping, exploitative, etc. I don't see any of that (except for depressing, maybe, but not all movies can be about happy circumstances, right?). I've not seen anyone make a good case of how the film exploits anyone, and the mere fact that it depicts abusive black characters in a ghetto doesn't \\"prove\\" that it's stereotyping, any more than a movie depicting abusive white characters is stereotyping. The unfortunate fact is that there is plenty of abuse going on in America. To be guilty of stereotyping, a book or film must imply that ALL people of a certain class or ethnicity behave in a certain way. This film does not do that. Also, let me suggest that if this film was about a white girl living in the ghetto, getting abused by white parents, it would be just as powerful, assuming that the white girl was as good an actress as Gabourey. :-)<br /><br />A minor problem I had with the film - and this is a personal quirk of mine - is that all of the \\"good\\" people were physically attractive. The teacher at the alternative school, the case worker, Nurse John. If this film is guilty of one stereotype, then that's it: Good people are good looking. Of course, the notable exception here is Precious herself, but I think the film subtly implies that Precious might not be obese if she had grown up in better circumstances. That being said, the teacher, and her girlfriend, and the case worker (Mariah Carey) were all beautiful, so I'm not complaining too much. It's a movie, after all. The thing is, I know that people who are beautiful inside come in all kinds of packages, and I think Hollywood sometimes forgets that. :-)s film does not do that. B0071K5KWU|5.0|In my 2004 Amazon review of Tony Banks' debut classical work, \\"Seven,\\" I wrote it marked \\"a new beginning for a very overlooked songwriter and composer.\\"  Thankfully, \\"Seven\\" was indeed this new beginning I was hoping for, and the subsequent result is what I was looking for and then some.  With \\"Six,\\" Banks now takes us deeper into the realm of classical music, augmenting his writing for orchestra with two stellar pieces of music for soloists.  This work again proves Banks is hardly in retirement with his great writing talent; as usual, he rewards listeners with a wide palate of tone color and imagination.<br /><br />The album opens with the most melodic and interesting work of the six.  In \\"Siren,\\" Banks employs saxophonist Martin Robertson (who has actually recorded sax on some of Banks' solo albums) to handle soloist duties.  Robertson's solo playing is very well paced and nicely compliments the orchestra.  I call it the most interesting work because it seems alto sax (or indeed any sax) is largely overlooked in classical music.  It was nice to hear it front and center in this work.<br /><br />The other work for soloist on the album is \\"Blade,\\" featuring violinist Charlie Siems.  While this up-and-coming musician is largely known to British audiences, Siems playing on this work is very impressive indeed, and should be a fine introduction to an American audience.  The crashing chord at the beginning of this work certainly catches your attention, anticipating greater things to come, and Siems does not disappoint.  Banks notes in his liner notes \\"Blade\\" could have worked as a rock song.  While there are hints of that flavor present, the piece still holds up nicely as classical fare.<br /><br />While much talk will probably be made of the soloists, the other pieces that complete the album are just as captivating.  \\"Still Waters\\" is a piece that starts off slow but progresses into something with surprising melodic depth and emotion.  Likewise, \\"Oracle\\" develops into abeautiful little tune.  \\"Wild Pilgrimmage\\" is a piece I wasn't particularly thrilled with at first, but subsequent listens have proven my first impression wrong.  There are quite a few subtle nuances in this piece that become more apparent on a second listen.<br /><br />The final work of the album, the epic \\"City of Gold,\\" is a long piece reminiscent of Banks' \\"Island of Darkness\\" from his \\"Strictly Inc.\\" album.  The adventurous feel to the music for this piece seems to set it apart from all of the other works, and it was a great choice to end the album on.  I couldn't help thinking though if there was more of a subtext to this piece.  A \\"City of Gold\\" is not particularly new imagery in the world of Genesis.  It made an appearance in the Genesis song, \\"Trick of The Tail,\\" which Banks also wrote: \\"Bored with his life in the City of Gold/he left and let nobody know.\\"  I wonder if perhaps this grander piece is a sequel or prequel to that earlier song.<br /><br />As with \\"Seven,\\" Banks showcases some interesting melodies and chord structures that make for a remarkable musical journey.  It would be interesting perhaps to see Banks attempt some other classical music landscapes with future projects.  A piece with piano coupled with a violinist or some other solo instrument (or even a voice) would make for an interesting idea.  So would a string quartet or piano quintet.  Whatever the end result, Banks' writing will most certainly make it something worth hearing.

B008602KQI|4.0|(this review contains no spoilers)<br /><br />Gary Ross's adaptation of THE HUNGER GAMES is pretty faithful -- in fact, it's probably one of the most faithful book-to-movie adaptations I've ever seen. I remember reading the first book in the series and constantly thinking \\"oh man, this would make a great movie.\\" And it's true: the movie version of THE HUNGER GAMES is good. However, most of the movie will seem very, very familiar to those that have already read the books. With original author Suzanne Collins cowriting the screenplay, many of the points in the movie are line-for-line translations to film.<br /><br />Here's some plot information for those that have not read the books: in the future, America has been segregated into 12 districts, and every year, two young adults are selected from each district (one male, one female). Those that are selected are entered into a competition called \\"The Hunger Games;\\" it's a competition where the winner is the last male or female left standing. In a dangerous environment with little food and plenty of weapons/traps, the main character, Katniss Everdeen, has to find a way to survive and make it back home.<br /><br />While I was skeptical of the cast, all of them turn in great performances. Jennifer Lawrence was fantastic in Winter's Bone, not so great in X-Men: First Class, but she really inhabits the role of Katniss Everdeen well. Elizabeth Banks isn't even recognizable while playing Effie Trinket. Stanley Tucci as Caesar Flickerman is an absolute blast. And on and on -- the cast is great.<br /><br />Gary Ross's direction is remarkably clear. I was pleasantly surprised by the dystopian aesthetic that the crew was able to achieve early in the movie. Once the characters find themselves in the Hunger Games, the setting is so wildly different -- and the same goes for the Capital. These settings never look cheap or uninspired: they are all different and relatively detailed. Some of the dialog doesn't quite translate well to the screen -- where characters spouting off exposition in the book feels natural, it comes off as a bit stilted here. The romance element doesn't really manifest itself all that well here either. One of the unfortunate things about this adaptation is how rigidly it adheres to its PG rating. The book is a bloody mess at times, and the movie is pretty sterile in comparison. While I understand that the studio essentially forced the movie to meet a PG-rating so that children would be able to to see it, the move really tones things down. A certain character's death isn't nearly as poignant because viewers aren't able to see everything that happens. Haymitch, a slobbering drunkard in the book, has a few drinks here, but isn't depicted nearly as disorderly as he does in the books.<br /><br />If you've read the books, your enjoyment of this movie will come from what you want from it: if you want a literal adaptation of the book, you are in for a treat because this movie never strays from the book. If, however, you would prefer the movie add something to the series that couldn't have been done in the books, you won't find much of it here. Overall, it's a pretty good movie, but the PG-rating removes some of the book's teeth.screen -- where characters spouting off exposition in the book feels natural, it comes off as a bit stilted here. A certain character's death isn't nearly as poignant because viewers aren't able to see everything that happens. Haymitch, a slobbering drunkard in the book, has a few drinks here, but isn't depicted nearly as disorderly as he does in the books.<br /><br />If you've read the books, your enjoyment of this movie will come from what you want from it: if you want a literal adaptation of the book, you are in for a treat because this movie never strays from the book. If, however, you would prefer the movie add something to the series that couldn't have been done in the books, you won't find much of it here. B0006B2A42|4.0|1968's \\"Ice Station Zebra\\" is a dramatization of the Alistair Maclean novel of the same name.  Its premise is the launching of an American nuclear attack submarine under the Arctic ice cap, loaded with Marines and two spies, one British, the other Russian.  Their objective is to rescue the crew of a drift ice science station, which has been damaged by fire.  In reality, they hope to retrieve a film capsule dropped from a satellite near Ice Station Zebra before the Soviets can get it.<br /><br />The cast was headlined by a number of prominent actors, including Rock Hudson as the commander of the USS TIGERFISH, Jim Brown as the leader of the Marines, Patrick McGoohan as a British intelligence officer, and Earnest Borgnine as a Russian spy who has defected to the West.  The plot is rather convoluted and involves multiple betrayals and a dramatic confrontation with the Soviets on the ice.  The ending is perhaps unexpected.  The cinematography and special effects have held up pretty well.<br /><br />\\"Ice Station Zebra\\" is still a decent action thriller of the Cold War genre, although it is not as good as MacLean's original novel, which featured a tense but coherent plot, an heroic American submarine crew, and a cunning but differently motivated hero.  Alistair Maclean fans are recommended to stick to the original; for all others, the movie is certainly worth a look.

B007IXWL2C|5.0|A lot of people have touched on this and I would like to defend the author for doing this; repeating the phrases and words both characters use: \\"Mr. Grey, Mrs. Grey \\" seems to be a particularly big issue with everyone. When we read the first book it was something Grey did that Ana picked up. It continued into the second book and then the third book because it is who they are, quirky and eclectic. If the author had changed some of the more frequent things they do or say, then we would lose who the characters really are by book two. If you ever take a literary class in school,  you will hear the word \\"repitition\\" throughout the entire course. It is a way to keep the audience in the loop throughout the story; it keeps everything fresh in your mind so you don't have to go flipping through pages to see if it was something already said or done and where. Yes there were some parts in the book where the word made absolutely no sense but as an avid reader and learner, I loved that they were new words I had never heard of before and that (after looking up the definition) they have now been added to my vocabulary. These types of books are meant to entertain,  not for you to completely over analyze like Ana. Please, just sit down and read. If you don't like it then don't read it but I love the books. I read a new book every day and I read this book quickly because I could not put it down,  which is a new for me. Great job E. L. James.

B003AMAOW4|5.0|Wagner's Lohengrin is perhaps his most overtly 'romantic' opera. Indeed, it is the quintessential 'knight in shining armor/damsel in distress' story. But like its other Wagnerian cousins (and direct relative, Parsifal) there are many layers of meaning at work in Wagner's text. Musically, this is his most brilliantly scored work, full of brassy fanfares and rousing choruses.<br /><br />And in every way, this is an outstanding musical performance. The American born and trained Kent Nagano, Director of the Bavarian State Opera, leads a performance that can stand comparison to any recording I have heard. His cast, many of them home grown favorites, is headed by THE great singer of our time, Jonas Kaufmann, whose voice, technique and temperment are ideally suited to the role of the Swan Knight.<br />Only one tentative attack on a pianissimo high note slightly mars an otherwise unimpeachable live performance, introspective, anguished, and heroic by turns.<br /><br />Anja Harteros, a German-Greek soprano whose star has been rising in recent years, plays Elsa as a visionary at odds with her regimented surroundings. She is fully immersed in the role and perfectly suited to the vocals demands, as well. Wolfgang Koch and Michaela Schuster are well matched as a thuggish Telramund and his blond gun-moll Ortrud. Friedrich is a notoriously ungrateful role, easily descending into stock moustache-twirling villainy and cruelly high in the voice. Koch is more than up to the vocal demands, and is wholly credible as the villain who is also a victim. Schuster, as his wife, is all eye-popping, sneering, malice. If she does not erupt with quite the volcanic force of Christa Ludwig, well, who else does?<br /><br />Christoff Fischesser, one of a bumper crop of young German basses, may seem a bit callow as the warrior-king Henry the Fowler (the one historic figure in the opera, father of the founfer of the Holy Roman Empire), but he sings Heinrich's demanding part with finesse and detail. TheKing's Herald, here a significant presence in acts one and two, is excellently proclaimed by Evgeny Nikitin<br /><br />The Bavarian State Opera Chorus is the equal of any, i.e. The Met or Vienna, opera chorus in the world, as is the Bavarian State Opera Orchestra.<br /><br />As to the production: To those \\"all-rude dank word-sayers\\" who decry anything resembling a modernized staging I say \\"Shut your mouth and open your mind!\\" I know every bar of this opera well, I own scores, both full orchestral and piano reduction, and I speak German fluently. I am also both a trained opera singer and trained in theater production. And I am telling you all that this is one of the most intelligent, well-thought-out modernized productions I have ever seen. Not a single detail is not clearly without meaning in the context the director, Richard Jones, has devised. Yes, perhaps we miss Lohengrin's entrance in gleaming armor. But if we understand him as man living a preternatural life, desperate to find his way to a normal world and fit in, then his appearance in a baggy t-shirt makes perfect sense. If he is not drawn onstage by the swan, he is at least carrying it, and this becomes an allusion to his father, Parsifal's slaying of a swan as an ignorant youth. The building of the house is a metaphor for the building of a Society, and when Lohengrin is betrayed he burns that house, leaving the town a series cots, like a homeless shelter.<br /><br />All aspects of Ultz's designs are similarly integrated: The Brabantian (Bavarian?) costumes gold uniforms yielding to Lohengrin's blue t-shirts; the wedding ceremony  mingling blue and gold sand in a vase; the Herald `broadcasting' from a tall chair to TV screens; even the quote from Wagner (Here, where my delusions find peace, I name Wahnfried, my home) integrate into a thoughtful gloss on Wagners score. To dismiss all regie-theater out of hand is as reactionary and luddite as those forgotten 19th Century critics who declared Wagner's \\"Musicof the Future\\" as an ill-fated aberration.

034552554X|5.0|The Language of Flowers falls into a genre I like to call \\"Book Club Lit\\".  It's that type of book that usually stars a broken young woman with a tragic past who has to has to learn to trust and forgive again, usually with the help of some special or quirky characters.  The Language of Flowers most definitely falls into that category, but I found it better than most in that genre.  It is a sad, yet hopeful book.  It begins on Victoria's 18th birthday as she is released from the foster system.  After spending all her life in the foster system, Victoria is almost feral and completely distrusts everyone.  She knows herself to be broken and unlovable.  The only time she felt loved was when was almost adopted by Elizabeth, who taught her the language of flowers (the Victorian notion that each flower conveys a meaning).  But she was taken away from Elizabeth at the age of 11 and put back into the foster system.  The reader finds out the details in the flashbacks interspersed between the present day.<br /><br />Victoria is an extremely damaged girl and her actions are not always rational.  Sometimes she is a hard protagonist to love and makes decisions that can infuriate the reader.  Victoria does start to heal, but in small doses, with a lot of set backs.  Ultimately though, she is seeking her own redemption and forgiveness and once she begins on that path she can start to accept the love of those around her.  Victoria's journey is messy and nothing is tied up with a pretty string, but you find yourself rooting for her the entire way.<br /><br />Language of Flowers is beautifully written - very lyrical and moody.  The beginning is amazing and really draws the reader in.  The middle tends to sag a bit, but it bolstered by the ending. I found this book to be a wonderful read overall.  And yes, it would be great for book clubs. Fans of books like Sarah's Key, Broken for You, or Water for Elephants should be all over this one.

0393340244|5.0|Cordelia Fine has done science a great service with \\"Delusions of Gender.\\"  Fine, a neurophilosopher of note, seeks to attack the now-prodigious belief that neuroscience has established the existence of \\"hard-wired\\" differences between the cognition of men and women.  In modern journalism and educational theory, it has become increasingly trendy to claim that neurological sex differences can explain anything from differing color preferences, to career choice, and even the gender gap in mathematics.  Some modern education theorists even claim that modern neurology supports the idea that education should be strictly sexually segregated in order to account for the supposed innate qualitative differences in learning ability between sexes(girls are supposedly inherently emphatic, while boys are supposedly inherently systemic).  These ideas are so casually cited as facts in the media that many people, in my experience at least, tend to have the impression that they represent the consensus of neuroscience.  Fine has three main objections to this popular trend:<br />1.  Neuroscientists have not, in fact, discovered real evidence of innately \\"hardwired\\" differences in the \\"knowledges\\" of males and females.<br />2. These pop writers misinterpret neuroscience in general.  The media has crafted a picture of neuroscience which portrays it as the search for innate human nature, which goes against the essence of the field, and creates the false impression that we should ignore data from the social sciences which points to the flexibility of gender roles.<br />3. Pop neuro-sexism has potentially negative effects, and already plays a role in discouraging women from attainment in mathematical fields, the corporate world, and other areas of life.  Unfortunately, pop neuro-sexism has begun to play the same social role as past biologically-based explanations of gender divides, in that it unthinkingly treats the fleeting present of society as the permanent state of nature.<br />As Fine stresses to the reader though, \\"Delusions of Gender\\" is not meant to refute the possibility that there may be some differences between the general neurological dispositions of men and women.  Rather, her goal is to demonstrate that there is overwhelming evidence that most of the gender gap is socially constructed, and that the notion that there are \\"male\\" and \\"female\\" brains is patently absurd.<br /><br />The book is broken up into three main sections.  The first collects the massive amount of sociological and psychological data which suggests that the much-discussed \\"math\\" gap and the gap in high-level economic attainment is socially constructed.  There have been multiple and oft-repeated studies which prove that women internalize the negative effects of sexism, which have nothing to do with their innate abilities.  When women are reminded of society's opinion on female analytic ability or business acumen, they tend to do very poorly on tests which measure these abilities.  However, when women are encouraged to believe that their gender shouldn't matter, or that women are actually good at traditionally \\"masculine\\" fields of knowledge, they far outperform the average female test-taker, even to the point where they nearly close the gender gap.  This poses a serious challenge to people who carelessly speculate that gender gaps can be explained by neurological difference: Even if there were different innate tendencies between men and women, what makes them so sure that our society has fully unleashed the analytical potential of the female half of the human race?  It's an assumption that I myself find frustrating, and it is somewhat confusing as to why neuro-sexists aren't called out on it more often in the media.<br /><br />Fine also points out that the real world itself flies in the face of our patriarchal assumptions.  If our gender gaps are so innate, then why have some countries entirely closed gaps in political representation and mathematical attainment?  In fact,there are some countries where girls outperform boys on math tests.  Even more interesting, high-ranking teams in the international math Olympics have an upward trend in female representation, to the point where many of the most successful teams are now gender egalitarian.  Today, half of all new Bachelor's degrees in mathematical fields are held by women.  Recently, Master's degree representation passed the 1/3 mark, and PhD representation continues to grow at a rapid rate.  Despite this, some male mathematicians still have the gall to claim that the Bachelors-Masters-PhD discrepancies represent an innate inferiority in mathematics, thereby arbitrarily throwing down yet another so-called \\"natural\\" barrier to women's attainment.<br /><br />The second section delves into neurological evidence, and a critique of the way in which neuroscience is used by neuro-sexists.  Fine explains the history of pop neuro-sexism, and describes the research claims of the handful of neurologists it has arbitrarily selected as its stars.  Fine respectfully disagrees with the actual neuroscientists in question, but blatantly takes glee in eviscerating the claims made in pop non-fiction books such as \\"Men are From Mars, Women are From Venus.\\"  She goes through a wide variety of commonly-cited pieces of evidence for neuro-sexism.  These include the in-utero testosterone spike in males, the emphatic tendencies of women, the supposed spatial superiority of \\"male\\" brains, and other such examples.  She presents many pieces of contrary evidence to the notion that these differences have the effects that the neuro-sexists claim they do, and explains why the ultimate effects of these phenomena on developing brains are still highly ambiguous.  Perhaps most importantly, she accuses pop neuro-sexists of having an entirely incorrect understanding of the brain.  Pop authors claim that brains have \\"emphatic\\" capacities and \\"systemic\\" capacities, and that the male-ness or female-ness of your brain determines which you have.  However, in doing this, they ignore the intricate interconnectivity of these capacities, and the ways in which they strengthen one another.  Furthermore, evidence suggests that just because a person tends to use one or the other does not even suggest a weakness in the other.  The human brain can be called upon to deploy both empathy and analytic ability.  It rarely ever shelves either of them, and can quickly shift in emphasis based on the situation at hand.  Also worthy of mentioning is that new evidence points to the possibility that having an emphatic emphasis may not cripple your scientific or mathematical ability at all, and even plays an essential role in genius-level systemic thought.<br /><br />Fine admits that there is some convincing evidence that there are some types of developmental differences which affect brain structure, such as the huge gap in rates of autism which exist between the sexes.  However, when looked at from a broad perspective, neurology suggests nothing resembling a gendered division of labor.  If anything, neurology suggests that the individual is the most important unit.  Traits which are present and variant within the individual cross the gender line.  Our socialization powerfully impacts our very neurological structure, and an individual is capable of rapidly shifting their neurological activity.  Pretending that sex discrimination is somehow respectful of the supposedly unique ways that \\"female brains think\\" is absurd, not to mention socially useless.<br /><br />The final section focuses on child development, and serves mainly as an objection to the common belief that childhood behavior represents raw, unmolded human nature.  Gendered socialization begins as soon as a baby is out of the womb, and as one study indicates, possibly even in-utero.  Even the most progressive parents treat their children differently in ways that they do not consciously recognize.  Sexism makes its way into parenting by the unconscious, yet pernicious use of different word choices and tones of voice, depending on the sex of the baby.  On top of this, childhood development research has proven that although it's nearly impossible to shake adherence to gender roles once they've been adopted, that there is a surprising degree of plasticity which can change depending on the attitudes of nearby adults and even the local culture of other children.  The point being, that socialization runs agonizingly deep, and the tendency of girls to wear pink (which is a very recent phenomenon) and for boys to associate child-rearing with being \\"sissy\\" (also more historical in nature than people tend to think) are by no means self-evidently natural.<br /><br />\\"Delusions of Gender\\" is a fresh and welcome rejoinder to the nonsense on stilts which presents itself as scientific objectivity.  The evidence I relayed here is barely a fraction of what Fine provides for the reader.  This book not only refutes specific points, but also provides an exciting picture of the complexities of modern neuroscience.  Fine's conversational tone makes for an excellent guide through the nitty-gritty of this burgeoning field, while also raising a strong objection to the pseudoscience which feeds off of it.  As Fine reminds the reader, despite cries of \\"political correctness\\" from pop neuro-sexists when faced with criticism, this issue has profound moral dimensions.  When people are told that they possess a biological trait that inhibits their mental abilities, it devastates their sense of ambition and confidence.  If neuro-sexists want to avoid moral criticism, then they should refrain from trying to reform our entire education system based on faulty science, or by actively trying to stem the tide of female attainment in the same exact manner as sexists have always done.  The author also suggests that neuro-sexism hurts males too, mainly through attempts by neuro-sexist education theorists to chalk up all \\"boys' problems\\" to their neurology, thus crowding out any discussion of the effects of cultural messages we as a society send boys during their development.ines which you have. B007MDB71O|4.0|It might surprise many people to find that many of our most revered adventure films hearken back to the era of the pulps for inspiration; the most glaring (and possibly best) example of that of course being the INDIANA JONES films. But there's also some brand-recognition that's at work at times to, more specifically with the recent (and amazingly awful) reboot of CONAN THE BARBARIAN. When director Andrew Stanton, director of two of the most beloved films in recent years (the beautiful FINDING NEMO and the Kubrick-with-a-heart genius of WALL-E, which I consider to be the best animated film ever made), decided to make his first jump into live-action films, he also looked back to the pulps. In this instance, he looked to the works of Edgar Rice Burroughs, the creator of Tarzan, and less-famously, the tale of a Confederate Civil War soldier thrust onto the Red Planet as originally told in the book \\"The Princess of Mars\\". While the film itself surrounds the adventures of the titular character, the real star of JOHN CARTER is Stanton and the deftness and joy that he tells this tale which, aside from a few missteps and some less-than-stellar characterization, is a rousing pulp sci-fi adventure extravaganza with some of the most eye-poppingly amazing visuals of a film since AVATAR.<br /><br />This film has a cast that is to die for... aside from its star. You have Lynn Collins as Dejah Thoris (The princess of the tale whose only other real claim to film is X-MEN ORIGINS: WOVERINE as Silverfox) who plays her as an intelligent, quirky, beautiful and deadly woman; Ciaran Hinds, James Purefoy and the voice of Polly Walker who all are there to bring the same charm or gravity that they brought to their respective characters in HBO's brilliant series ROME; Dominic West (best known from THE WIRE) as Sab Than, the would-be puppet tyrant of Barsoom; Mark Strong (the British heavy in just about every film from SHERLOCK HOLMES to GREEN LANTERN) as Matai Shang, the immortal who looks to control the fate of Barsoom; Bryan Cranston in an unrecognizable but important role; and the voice talents of Willem Dafoe (who also worked with Stanton on FINDING NEMO) as Tars Tarkas, Samantha Morton (MINORITY REPORT) as Sola, and Thomas Haden Church as Tal Hajus.<br /><br />Taylor Kitsch as Carter lacks a certain consistency in his characterization. He has charm and is ruggedly handsome, but he lacks the leading-man charisma a role like this requires. The writing by Stanton, Michael Chabon, and Mark Andrews, who have a number of characters to deal with, a great deal of plot and exposition and to combine all of this with some bravura action set-pieces hurts the film somewhat in terms of characterization and may seem like a waste of talent in some cases, but each actor has their moment to shine, no matter the size of the part. The real winner, cast-wise, is Collins, who is so strong in her performance that I want to see her in more roles, and would love to see a film based just around her character. But the film is strong enough on its other merits to maintain great interest in the characters and the story to see it all the way through and still have a terrific time.<br /><br />The biggest winner here is sadly the biggest loser of everyone involved here, and that is Andrew Stanton. He directs the film with an eye for spectacle, precision of detail, and most appropriately, a strong sense of fun; the best example of this is the scene in which Carter leaps from ship to ship eventually meeting and fighting alongside Dejah. It's an incredible moment in the film because of just how well each moment is orchestrated. He tells the story of the film with a visual assuredness that rivals that of more experienced live-action filmmakers, and the filmmakers assembled a fantasic crew of digital and practical effects artists so that the interaction between the human/humanoid actors and the CG characters is totally seamless. Unfortunately, the film was something of a colossal bustat the box office due to exceedingly luke-warm reviews, an epically poor marketing campaign, and the natural comparisons between this film and AVATAR. I would watch this film a hundred more times before I would ever even think of watching AVATAR again, but it's a comparison worth making. So because of all this, the \\"failure\\" of this film to reach the heights it was intended to hit is all on Stanton's shoulders, which may hurt his ability to continue making live-action films. Although, looking at the reviews on Amazon, this film might receive some new life on home video, and the disconnect that exists between the marketing of the film and getting audiences in the theaters may now start to dissipate via word of mouth.<br /><br />When all is said and done, JOHN CARTER is a exciting pulp-flavored visual feast with something for everyone, and I can only hope that everyone can watch it and enjoy it as much as I did.<br /><br />4/5 Stars.at the box office due to exceedingly luke-warm reviews, an epically poor marketing campaign, and the natural comparisons between this film and AVATAR. 0553801473|4.0|A Dance With Dragons is essentially A Feast for Crows, Part II.  Both books take place immediately after the (insane) events of A Storm of Swords, and both books feature fewer major events than their predecessors.  A Dance with Dragons emerges as the better of the two due to its point-of-view characters being those more central to the series' overall story.<br /><br />A Dance with Dragons has been criticized for being overlong, with \\"not much happening.\\"  Some reviews express the opinion that Martin's world has grown too large and his characters too spread out; many reviewers doubt that he will be able to bring them back together for a satisfying conclusion in the final two books.  I disagree.  It's true that this book doesn't feature the earth-shattering events of A Storm of Swords, but its description of the aftermath of these events is clearly moving the series toward its conclusion.  On the Wall, the Free Folk are being welcomed as allies against the greater threat of the Others.  In Mereen, Daenerys struggles with her abilities as a ruler, and she finally gets to use--albeit in a limited sense--those lovely lizards of hers.  In and near King's Landing, Varys and a young man with his sights on the throne complicate the Lannisters' plan to salvage the realm.  All of these events are important to the main plots of the series: the Others' return, Daenerys' journey home, and the Westeros \\"game of thrones.\\"<br /><br />Are events moving as quickly as some would like?  Perhaps not.  But, to be fair, the series has had to change course multiple times in only a few volumes, and Martin is not unwise in taking the time to explore the consequences of his previous books.  Before a certain execution in the first book, I would never have anticipated the large-scale civil war that occurred in the second book.  Similarly, the events of the Red Wedding essentially ended the War of the Five Kings, so the fourth and fifth books take the form of the remaining pieces aligning themselves for the battles to come.  \\"Should it take two full volumes to do so?\\" I hear critics cry.  It's a fair criticism, but I think it's one that applies more to 'Feast' than 'Dance.'  The experiences of Cercei, Brienne, and Jaime, while interesting from a character perspective, probably could have been edited down significantly without impacting the main story.  The characters of 'Feast' just aren't as critical to the story as Daenerys, Tyrion, and Jon Snow, and, in retrospect, it might have been better to combine the two volumes into a single work.<br /><br />This observation doesn't diminish the value of the fifth book itself, which stands on its own as a fine addition to the series.  There are wonderful opportunities for characters to grow in this volume, from Jon Snow's \\"Kill the boy\\" to Theon's character turnaround to Daenerys' confronting her own fallibility as a queen.  If Martin can make me root for Theon Greyjoy again, his storytelling prowess is clearly still strong.  It's true that Martin makes too much use of the \\"character-in-peril cliffhanger,\\" but we had these in book two (Bran and Rickon, Tyrion) and book three (Arya), and, while annoying, they are infrequent enough that they don't greatly detract from the book's overall impact.  Yes, there were no major battles in this book, but neither was there in A Game of Thrones.  As with the first book in the series, A Dance with Dragons is all about character development, and it thrives because of this.  The pieces are in place for The Winds of Winter to be filled with the spectacular action sequences that fans of the series love.  I can't wait!

B000002A3T|5.0|After many years of enjoying Pink Floyd's music, I feel the Division Bell may indeed be their best album.  The Division Bell grows on you, with each listen, and all the right ingredients of great music and lyrics are there to make it an enjoyable experience.  I understand there is a lot of disagreement, among Pink Floyd fans, over who carries Pink Floyd's legacy, although the Division Bell further proves that David Gilmour, Richard Wright and Nick Mason made excellent music, together, without the input of Roger Waters.<br />  On July 6, 1977, I was fortunate enough to see Pink Floyd play at Montreal's Olympic Stadium during the Animals tour.  At the time, Dark Side of the Moon and Wish You Were Here were still all the buzz in most people's heads, since Animals had just been released and was not yet as known as those previous two albums.  Imagine that I was able to attend that concert in Montreal for $25, after purchasing a ticket in Plattsburgh, NY which included bus service to and from the concert, as well as admission to see the show.  In addition, there was a keg of beer also provided, in the rear of the bus, which made the trip to Montreal that day all the more memorable.  Back in those days, crossing the border, into Canada, was as simple as declaring one's citizenship and how long you planned to be there, but I don't believe the border guards even bothered to come onto the bus, because everyone knew Pink Floyd was going to be playing in Montreal and by the sound coming from the bus it was probably evident where we were going!  The concert was general admission and me and a friend found ourselves sitting on the grass around 400 yards in front of stage.  There were people walking around, with their faces painted, and all types of drugs being shared and/or consumed, as mental preparation for Pink Floyd to take the stage was highly anticipated by all.  A distinct white cloud of smoke hovered over the crowd, emitted from the collective sharing of joints that circulated among the over 90,000 people in Olympic Stadium that day.  The weather was perfect and no one was thinking who was the best member of Pink Floyd, or what turn their music would take in the future, because many, including myself, had also heard Meddle, Ummagumma or Obscured by Clouds and knew this band was always capable of producing quality music, even after the bar had been set so high with the release of Dark Side of the Moon.  Even David Bowie's version of See Emily Play on his Pinups album was a compliment to the band, since Mick Ronson was such a great guitarist.<br />  As the concert got underway, the crowd was treated to the amazing live performance of nearly the entire Dark Side of the Moon and Wish You Were Here Albums with songs from Animals including in the mix.  At one point in the show, a sixty foot helium pig was inflated and began its slow trek high over the crowd in Olympic stadium, but someone in the crowd fired a bottle rocket and the pig was sent back to the stage area and deflated.  An exploding helium balloon would not be welcome by anyone and I understand it was during this concert that Roger Waters began to feel there was a Wall separating himself from the audience.  The seeds for the next album were now being sown and no one but Roger Waters truly knows whether it was his ego or paranoia that forced him away from the band.  Now, 35 years later, I feel the Wall was when Roger Waters showed the rest of us that he began to take himself too seriously and a division was also created between himself and the other members of Pink Floyd.  The usual tempo and flow of music that makes Dark Side of the Moon and Wish You Were Here, as well as Animals, such enjoyable albums began to fade with the release of The Wall.  I understand it was Roger's insistence on playing all the instruments, as well as writing the lyrics, that created the greatest division among members of Pink Floyd and we the fans must ask ourselves if it wasn't a good thing that DavidGilmour wanted to prove his contributions to Pink Floyd by carrying on their music.  The best song on The Wall is Comfortably Numb written and song by who?  The same man who continued to carry the Pink Floyd banner, with Momentary Lapse of Reason and The Division Bell, with the help of Richard Wright and Nick Mason.  If you listen to Pros and Cons of Hitch Hiking you will hear the point I'm making, carried to extreme, with Roger Water's screaming.  David Gilmour is not capable of such nonsense and The Division Bell is a return to form for Pink Floyd.  I only recently purchased The Division Bell, providing myself a time capsule of enjoyment, because I've always enjoyed the solo albums of Richard Wright and David Gilmour so much.  Richard Wright's Wet Dream is pure pleasure and David Gilmour's three solo albums are equally great as his contributions with Pink Floyd.<br />  As a side note, I also saw Emerson, Lake and Palmer at the Olympic Stadium, later that same year, as they were accompanied by an 80 piece orchestra, which was only used in Montreal and New York, before being dropped because it was too expensive.  I arrived at the Emerson, Lake and Palmer concert in Montreal on another bus trip from Plattsburg except this time I only paid $18.50!

This one's a good song.<br /><br />8. \\"Lyin' Eyes\\"- This one isn't the best one here, but it is another Judas Priest-esque song. It isn't a favorite of mine, but it does rock and have a driving guitar part. Others may like it more, but to me, it's not that bad, but I'm not thrilled by it either.<br /><br />9. \\"Dreamer\\"- Ok, is it just me, or does the piano intro and the chorus of this song remind you of Infinity-era Journey? I just think it has that 70's Steve Perry Journey ring to it. Anyway, this song is a ballad and both \\"Open Your Heart\\" and this one are GREAT ballads. I like \\"Open Your Heart\\" more in terms of vocal work, but I like the musical work on this song better. This one is mellower and has less power, but it's more of a relaxing song. Great song none the less!<br /><br />10. \\"Dance the Night Away\\"- This one is a party type song and closes this album off. I'm not too big of a fan of it, but like I keep saying, it's not outta place, it's just a difference in opinion from person to person. It does have some rather cool guitar stuff going on, but I just don't care for it.<br /><br />Verdict: This album is great for fans of: Judas Priest (I'm not a fan, but a lot of this sounds similar to 80's Priest), Iron Maiden (Not really cause it sounds like it, but because it has a metal influence in it), Journey, and other 80's bands of this sort. I haven't heard all of TFC album, but I've listened to some of it, then found other things to listen to while this one kept my attention. It just has a better quality to it than typical Commercial Europe. Great album and highly recommended!

Technically, it is a 1997 movie, but it was released in the U.S. two years later as part of the contract between Disney and Studio Ghibli. I missed the opportunity to catch it in theaters back in 99, but I got the chance to see it a year later on DVD.    All I knew at that time was that it was made by Hayao Miyazaki, who had only two previous films released in the U.S.: MY NEIGHBOR TOTORO and KIKI'S DELIVERY SERVICE. I heard stories about how his work is an inspiration to animators at both Disney and Pixar, and that he was a mentor to John Lasseter. I knew nothing about the story or the characters, and that was an advantage in this kind of a film.    I'll try not to reveal too much about the story. Set in ancient time when forests were ruled by gods, mankind has been doing terrible damage to the forests and have been angering the gods. When a boar god becomes a murderous demon and attacks a village a young boy named Ashitaka successfully defeats the demon, but at a terrible price. His right arm comes in contact with the demon and he is inflicted with a curse that will eventually kill him. He is the last of his tribe, and is told by the village's wise  woman to go on a journey to find a cure. Cutting his hair, he sets off on his journey, vowing to see the world without hate.    Ashitaka saves the life of two men  who live and work in a village known as Iron Town. The town is ruled by a woman named Lady Eboshi. Her people have been cutting down trees in order to manufacture Iron, and have been fighting with the gods with deadly weapons. A human girl named San who was raised by a wolf god has vowed to kill Lady Eboshi and has a deep hatred for all humans, including Ashitaka when he gets in the way of an attack she makes on Eboshi.    If this story seems rather oversimplified to you, believe me, it is not. In reality, there isn't really any clear line about who is good and who is evil in this film. Lady Eboshi isn't an evil character. She is a woman with some flawed motivation. She is actually seen as a good person to the people of Iron Town--someone who rescued young prostitutes and given them a much better working and living environment. Another character is Jigon: a man who is simply out to make money.    What Miyazaki demonstrates in this movie is that he creates characters that get your attention, and images that are just plain astonishing. He personally draws thousands of frames himself, which is something that most animation directors don't do. With PRINCESS MONONOKE, he does something that very few films, animated or not, are able to accomplish for me. He makes me forget that I'm watching a movie, and makes it feel like I'm actually in this movie. It's like an out of the body experience.    I cannot describe these amazing and really beautiful images that PRINCESS MONONOKE gives us. But I can reveal two things.  Many times during the movie there are scenes where you hear absolutely nothing on the soundtrack: no music, no dialogue, no special effects. The image will actually stop for several seconds, without any camera movement. Quiet scenes like this are so rare these days, especially in animated films. Miyazaki understands that not every scene has to have constant movement or quick cutting, like in most Hollywood action movies. PRINCESS MONONOKE does have several action scenes in it, but it is action that you can actually care about.    The other thing I can tell you about is the ending. There is a romance in the movie, but this isn't a romance where the hero gets the girl. It is actually one where the hero doesn't get the girl YET! Two of the characters love each other, but they understand that they cannot live together. When this movie ended, I had the sense that a real romance would actually come between them, but it would simply take time. How many movies, animatedor not, have the courage to end that way?    There is another element in this wonderful movie. The music is by Miyazaki's longtime composer: Joe Hisashi. He has created a beautiful and mesmerizing score for this movie. It adds to the emotional power of the story and images, something so very few movies are able to accomplish.    PRINCESS MONONOKE was the film that sparked my interest in Hayao Miyazaki, and I currently own all of his movies on DVD. It also got me interested in other Japanese Anime. What the Japanese understand is that animation does not have to be pigeonholed as entertainment for children. PRINCESS MONONOKE has scenes that are dark and violent. It is rated PG-13, and I consider that rating appropriate. If there are still some children over 13 who still have the childlike wonder in them and haven't lost it due to the cinematic ADD that too many Hollywood movies generate, PRINCESS MONONOKE will be a real treasure.    The great filmmaker Howard Hawkes had a formula for defining a great movie: \\"Three great scenes, no bad scenes.\\" Multiply that by three and you get PRINCESS MONONOKE.    note: Hayao Miyazaki made the best film for children that I've ever found: MY NEIGHBOR TOTORO. This should be the very first film children see in their lifetimes. If this review has sparked an interest in Miyazaki for you I also recommend a wonderful book about this man: HAYAO MIYAZAKI, MASTER OF JAPANESE ANIMATION by Helen McCarthy, which is available here at Amazon.com    UPDATE, 2015: PRINCESS MONONOKE has, at long last, been released on blu-ray. The best movie of 1999 just got better!!!!!! This blu-ray is absolutely gorgeous. And the new DTS 5.1 sound is just plain wonderful.

B002FL5HYQ|4.0|If you're considering reading a Trump book, there is no doubt you know he is successful. Likely, you also know that he has an ego the size of Trump Tower. Well then, who better to write a book called \\"Think Big and Kick Ass\\"? Trump's personality comes through boldly in the book. Sometimes it is humorous and motivating, other times it can be a little much and get off-topic slightly.<br /><br />The book is co-written with Bill Zanker, founder of The Learning Annex, an online channel for self improvement. Bill's commentary are at the end of each chapter and are only a couple pages per chapter. What this adds to the book is another point of view for each of Donald's major points.<br /><br />The book is broken down into 10 chapters, with a summary of Key Points at the end of each chapter, which I think is very helpful when you want a little refresher. There is also a list of books for recommended reading in the Appendix and a Q&A of common questions Trump has received during speeches with the Learning Annex.<br /><br />Alas, here are the chapters and my commentary on each one:<br /><br />1. Do You Have What It Takes?<br />Can you really dream big? If so, there is a multiple-choice test in this chapter to self-assess. This sets the tone for the book. If you don't want to be super successful and aren't willing to bust your hump to get there, don't continue reading.<br /><br />2. Passion, Passion, Passion!<br />Do what you love. For Trump, it is real estate and making deals. Maybe for you it is teaching others or baking. Just start doing what makes you happy and take action every day to get bigger and better.<br /><br />3. Basic Instincts<br />Go with your instinct, using all knowledge that you have.<br /><br />4. Creating Luck<br />I was actually surprised that Trump acknowledged and made some thoughtful points regarding luck. Some people do get lucky and some people get tough times. However, he rightfully tells you to avoid thinking of the world in only these terms. Create your own luck by working hard and putting yourself in the best situations. Mind over matter. Action over standing still.<br /><br />5. Fear Factor<br />What I wanted to be my favorite chapter turned out to be my least favorite. He went a little too far off topic on this one. My only takeaway was to realize that people want to go after the #1 and replace them. That means on your way to the top people will try to knock you off and once you get there, you have a target on your back. Essentially, be on the lookout and be aggressive, and don't let others dissuade you.<br /><br />6. Revenge<br />Funniest chapter by far. To quote Donald's summary: \\"When somebody screws you, screw them back in spades.\\"<br /><br />7. Big Mo!<br />An object in motion tends to stay in motion. Get going! And once you do, keep the momentum and don't rest on your laurels.<br /><br />8. Never Take Your Eye Off The Ball<br />A continuation in thought from the previous chapter. Keep your focus.<br /><br />9. I Love You, Sign This<br />Get a prenup. No exceptions. There is no room for error or doubt in the back of your mind about future of the business you are building.<br /><br />10. Thing Big and Kick Ass in Business and Life<br />This is the \\"bring it all together\\", high-level chapter. If you're familiar with the movie Boiler Room, act \\"as if\\". Live the life you think a successful person would live and put yourself in the situations and environments that a successful person would. To a good degree, I like what he is saying. Although I would argue that one shouldn't be irresponsible by living too far beyond their means if they have to support others.<br /><br />Here's what I think: if you need a little motivation and like the lifestyle of the Donald, you'll probably like this book. I would recommend borrowing from a friend or from the library (that's what I did) to determine whether or not this is a book that you need to buy to keep on your shelf. If you've read books from Napoleon Hill and DavidSchwartz, you might skip this one and start taking action on your goals instead.

B005MYEQ4U|4.0|MIDNIGHT IN PARIS, in some ways, reminds me of that Twilight Zone episode, \\"The Trouble With Templeton,\\" in which an aging stage actor, nostalgic for his youth, ends up traveling to his past. Like that episode, there are wistful elements that suffuse this film's narrative. Our rear view mirror tends to be rose-tinted, isn't it? Gil Pender (Owen Wilson) and his fianc&eacute;e Inez (Rachel McAdams) are holidaying in Paris, sort of mooching off Inez's parents who are there on a business trip. Gil, a self-confessed hack scriptwriter who's raring to write his great American novel, is enamoured with La Ville-Lumi&egrave;re, the City of Light. On and on he goes regaling a couldn't care less Inez with anecdotes concerning the literary luminaries who populated Paris back in the day. Inez's eyes are gone all glazed. She'd rather go shopping. So, from jump, the dynamics of their relationship set off our uh-oh radar. And yet MIDNIGHT IN PARIS is touted partly as a frothy romantic comedy.<br /><br />It's written and directed by Woody Allen, and you know what you're getting with that name brand. Clever words, a touch of satire, a slice of whimsy, and a neurosis or two. I hear Owen Wilson delivering his dialogue, his verbal tics and cadence, and I picture Woody Allen mouthing along those same lines. I didn't like Wilson's character initially; he seemed a little pushy and opinionated. But then along comes Gil's fianc&eacute;e's old crush, a gent who is so pedantic and know-it-all that Gil, next to him, immediately becomes more sympathetic. After one evening of wine tasting, Mr. Pedantic extends an invitation to Gil and Inez to step out and go dancing; Inez accepts and Gil declines. Gil instead opts for a midnight stroll. And when he gets lost and sits on the steps of that old church, along sidles an old classic Peugeot packed with revelers who wave Gil on over. A bemused Gil gets in the Peugeot and off they motor to the swinging 1920s.<br /><br />No explanation at all for the time travel. Woody Allen expects us to just roll with it, and I was cool with that. In the dizzy era of jazz and flappers, a flabbergasted Gil bumps into one literary icon after another, from F. Scott Fitzgerald (and his wild wife Zelda) to Ernest Hemingway to Gertrude Stein (warmly played by Kathy Bates). And maybe the high point of Gil's evening takes place in Gertrude Stein's salon when she offers to proofread his manuscript.<br /><br />Meanwhile, life in the present day keeps on keeping on. Except that now Gil's fianc&eacute;e and her parents have taken to wondering where Gil disappears to when evening swings around. The future pops-in-law even sics a private detective to shadow him (the gumshoe's eventual fate may elicit a grin). In the roaring '20s, after each midnight, Gil keeps running into famous and interesting folks (including Adrien Brody's entertainingly manic Salvador Dali). And, in the past, Gil meets the exquisite Adriana (Marion Cotillard) who once was mistress to Braque and Modigliani and is currently Picasso's lover. Surprisingly, sparks fly between her and Gil. And I did mention \\"frothy romantic comedy,\\" didn't I?<br /><br />Every now and then a Woody Allen film muddles that line separating fantasy and reality. Two of my favorite Woody Allen films are [[ASIN:0792846117 Sleeper]] and [[ASIN:B00005O06L The Purple Rose of Cairo]], with MIDNIGHT IN PARIS perhaps elbowing in and inching up in my personal rankings. I love this premise; I love time travel. Paris looks exquisite, day or night. Woody Allen fabricates grace and wit and romance, not just of the heart but of an era. The ensemble cast is fantastic. Marion Cotillard is enchanting and luminous (but when is she not?). Owen Wilson's character exhibits such enthusiasm over the Who's Who of Paris circa 1920s that he becomes just irrepressible. Note that Woody isn't going for out-and-out authenticity with his depiction of the historical figures. He's having too good a time exaggerating a trait, tweakinga characteristic. I chuckled at Papa Hemingway's tendency to relate everything to one's masculinity and virility. I enjoyed Gil's befuddling of Luis Bu&ntilde;uel. I will say that you'll get more out of this film if you're familiar with even a few of the larger than life personalities Gil meets in the past. But if you were in the dark with some of these folks - as I was, occasionally - well, I say this: to the wiki!<br /><br />The DVD's bonus feature:<br /><br />- \\"Midnight in Cannes\\" - at the MIDNIGHT IN PARIS press conference in the Cannes Film Festival, 2011 (00:04:57 minutes)<br />- Theatrical Trailera characteristic. 1593083564|4.0|Mansfield Park is my fourth Jane Austen novel (Sense and Sensibility, Pride and Prejudice, and Persuasion being the others read).  For two thirds of the story, I thought to myself that Mansfield Park was going to be my favorite Jane Austen novel.  It is longer than the other three novels I have read with various plot lines that kept me interested in what was going to happen next.  But then it seemed to me that Jane Austen decided it was time to bring her long story to an end.  I was uncomfortable with the speed with which she resolved the many problems that had kept the reader wondering how everything would turn out.<br /><br />Fanny Price is our main character and she is much like Cinderella.  Taken at ten years old from her poor home to live with rich relatives, she is given an unheated attic bedroom and then treated as little better than a servant by her adopted family, except for her beloved cousin Edmond who realizes Fanny's true worth.  Eight years later when the main action of the story begins, Fanny is now a beautiful and demure young woman who genuinely cares for her family and friends and serves them as best she can.  She seems almost too good to be true, but from time to time her humanity reveals it self when she shows herself to be a harsh critic who expects others to be perfect, like herself.  Her cousin Edmond is her male counterpart.  The two cousins sit in judgment on the people in their little world who do not live up to their high standards.<br /><br />Fortunately for the reader, the rest of the characters in the novel have faults and failings.  Henry and Mary Crawford are the two foils for Fanny and Edmond and both are living, breathing human beings who do not live up to the high expectations of Fanny and Edmond.  Henry proposes marriage to Fanny and she rejects him utterly.  His proposal itself is an affront to her because she thinks him a shallow flirt.  Edmond wants to propose to Mary, but he thinks she will not accept him because he intends to become a clergyman and he she has contempt for men of the cloth.  These are two major plot lines in the novel and Austen resolves them with dispatch, disposes of other loose ends, and then ends her story.<br /><br />Critics rate Jane Austen at the top of the list of great English novelists.  She continues to be read and admired and many films have been made of her work.  Pride and Prejudice with Colin Firth as D'Arcy is the best of them, in my opinion, and Mansfield Park, with Frances O'Connor as Fanny Price, takes so many liberties with the original story that most Jane Austen fans will resent this retelling of the novel.<br /><br />Each of us has our own favorites when it comes to English novelists and I must confess that Anthony Trollope and Charles Dickens are at the top of my list.  That said, I agree with the critics that Jane Austen's work continues to deserve an audience and I recommend Mansfield Park as representative of her work.become a clergyman and he she has contempt for men of the cloth. That said, I agree with the critics that Jane Austen's work continues to deserve an audience and I recommend Mansfield Park as representative of her work.

B0016K40KO|5.0|If David Lean's \\"The Bridge on the River Kwai\\" is any indication, it's that characterization and storytelling can be as beautiful as a striking landscape. These days, most Hollywood epics are either big-budget fluff pieces or overstuffed slogs that are rich in visuals and poor in content. \\"The Bridge on the River Kwai\\" is a real epic: epic in themes, settings, characterizations and ideas.<br /><br />\\"Bridge\\", more than many movies of its time and today, is a startling portrait of the madness of war. The movie may be about a captured British unit forced to build a bridge for the Japanese army and that the British Army sends a unit of commandos to sabotage it but it's all a McGuffin to reveal how war and illusions of grandeur profoundly affect the human psyche. Alec Guinness' Nicholson and Sessue Hayakawa's Saito, two opposing sides of the coin, both want to build a bridge for different reasons: for Nicholson, it is to show the ingenuity and hard work of British soldiers, despite the fact that it will aid the enemy, even going as far as sending physically damaged soldiers to do the job; for Saito, it is to express Japanese pride or commit harakiri. The bridge, far from symbolizing the human connection, symbolizes the futility of war and how impossible visions and baseless loyalty lead to the disintegration of the human mind.<br /><br />Adding an interesting twist to this story is the character of Shears, superbly played by William Holden. Shears escapes the Japanese base camp and is miraculously rescued, only to be forced by the British army to go back and sabotage the bridge or he will be charged for impersonating the officer. Shears is a soldier trying but failing to escape the horrors of war and that the British army's refusal to heed to his pleas reveals how any country's declaration that a soldier performs his duty to defend his country are a baseless proclamation in the face of reality.<br /><br />Watching \\"Bridge\\" for the first time, one can easily be misled into thinking that the movie is a grand, old-fashioned action-adventure. But the movie is more than just an adventure. Like David Lean's other majestic epic, \\"Lawrence of Arabia\\", \\"Bridge\\" is a movie filled with ideas, revealed upon multiple viewings by examining the cinematic details, from the encompassing landscapes to the chose of camera angles depicted.<br /><br />Speaking of which, David Lean's command of the cinematography in \\"Bridge\\" is absolutely spectacular. There are, of course, the epic shots like the deep focus shots in the camp where Shears talk about his escape plan with the British unit in the background, the extreme long shots of the commandos scaling the cliffs with Warden limping in the foreground and the shots of the bridge's construction and eventual completion. But its subtle moments are equally memorable, such as the claustrophobic shots of Nicholson in the oven, the full shot of Saito crying after releasing Nicholson and the officers and the concluding montage of Nicholson's realization of \\"what he has done\\" before purposely (or is it accidentally?) destroying the bridge. One of my personal favorite shots in the movie are not even epic shots but a simple low-angle shot of an escaped Shears' head looming over the hot sun, an expression of fatigue and desperation presented from the man's face. Shots like these prove that there was more to Lean's epics than camels and lush romances.<br /><br />Anyone accustomed to the fast-paced, rapidly-edited action movies of Christopher Nolan and Paul Greengrass may be put off by the movie's deliberate pacing but that really works in the movie's favor. It doesn't jump into anything; it takes its time and sets up a chain of events that reaches an emotionally satisfying conclusion. Despite its 160 minute length, \\"Bridge\\" is hardly boring. It is eternal proof that a movie can still rivet an audience without a dozen action scenes linked together.<br /><br />\\"The Bridge on the River Kwai\\" works on many levels: as a taut action-adventure, as a powerful psychological study of men in a noncombat war situation and as a grand epic with big shots and bigger themes. The two-disc special edition is the DVD/Blu-Ray to get. Watch it and you'll marvel at the skill, ambitions and passion that made \\"Bridge\\" one of the most memorable war movies of the 1950s.<br /><br />Strongest recommendation to buy.aut action-adventure, as a powerful psychological study of men in a noncombat war situation and as a grand epic with big shots and bigger themes. The two-disc special edition is the DVD/Blu-Ray to get. Watch it and you'll marvel at the skill, ambitions and passion that made \\"Bridge\\" one of the most memorable war movies of the 1950s.<br /><br />Strongest recommendation to buy.

B00004Z4SD|5.0|From the day this film came out in theaters I have been watching it regularly. I watch it about every year and a half. It is one of Lynch's better films and if it were not for Elephant Man would maybe be his best. Although many Lynch fans may disagree with me on that one. Few films have been able to accomplish what this movie has. It is a study on the notion of forgiveness and importance of family. It's about a lonely pilgrimage which really plays on an important part of Lynch's personal life. Lynch has said on occasion that one of his favorite things to do is just sit in a chair and think (Lynch is also big on meditation). Alvin Straight, \\"It's amazing what you see when you are just sitting.\\" Lynch used to go to a restaurant to sit and look at the silverware, think and write on the napkins because \\"Ideas are the best things going.\\" Alvin is a walking contradiction which is all resolved by the fact that he is riding a lawn mower 500 miles. How is this possible? The answers is in the stars. If you like action movies then this is not going to work for you at all. This is more for the student of film and the person who likes to think. I would not disagree with the person who says that the movie is not entertaining. It's a slow movie. It's about an old man driving a lawnmower for goodness sake! It captures the feeling of the slow country life. There are long distance shots that really make you feel like you are familiar with the landscape. There are only a few times in which the movie makes you feel like you are riding the mower with Alvin, which was a good decision. If you are simply emotionally moved by this film then I think you have probably missed the point and maybe some of the finer details. Yes it is emotionally moving but it is more than that. It is a series of pictures pieced together to tell one tale from many different angles. Lynch took this so seriously he wouldn't allow chapters in the final DVD release. Why did he allow chapters in his other films? Because he needs you to watch it in one sitting to get what is going on. Amazing film. I have endeavored not to \\"over think\\" this movie. Think too hard when watching Mulholland  Drive and in my opinion you are wasting your time because it's a television pilot with an ending pasted on not unlike the Twin Peaks Pilot made for the Japanese market. The Straight Story can only be observed well or not. If you are thinking too hard then you are missing its meaning. This film is a huge departure from Lynch's artistic philosophy. Commonly he would prefer the viewer create or derive their own meaning like a moving painting (Six Men Getting Sick). But with Straight Story he has crafted a work of genius with clear meaning. Mary Sweeney (co-writer) hoped he would fall in love with the project and that he would direct it which is exactly what happened. Thank God for her influence on the film, she deserves a lot of credit.
